A growing number of technology conglomerates provide lending services to shoppers who frequent their e-commerce marketplaces. Technology firms have the information advantage that commercial banks lack. A vast amount of proprietary digital footprints are now at their fingertips. Study shows the information content of proxies for human behavior, lifestyle, and living standard in the non-transnational data are just effective, hence highly valuable for default prediction (Berg et al. 2020). However, managing retail credit risks in online marketplaces differs in one fundamental way from managing credit-card default risks faced by commercial banks. It comes from the pronounced “action-response” relationship between the lender’s credit decisions and the borrowers’ delinquency outcomes. When customers apply for credit to finance their online purchases, they effectively enter into an unsecured loan contract where the counterparty is the platform lender, and they are expected to make installments according to the payment schedules. These loan decisions, especially the loan amount and loan interest, are far more individualized than those made in the traditional credit card business and are frequently adjusted. The e-commerce lenders observe that their credit policies have a systematic causal impact, which alters borrowers’ payment behavior, irrespective of age, income, education, occupation, and so forth.

It is conceivable that machine learning (ML) algorithms can effectively tap into the information advantage for accurate estimations of delinquency rate (Khandani, Kim, and Lo 2010). However, the existing retail credit risk studies do not recognize the essential of action-response causalities as far as we know. Indeed, obtaining the accurate estimation of delinquency rate does not mean that we can obtain the accurate estimation of the action-response causality. Generally, we face two biases in estimating the action-response causality. The first bias is due to the neglect of the confounding effects. In reality, loan decisions are the results of the lender’s decision algorithms that use an overlapping set of borrowers’ features with those used for risk assessments, e.g., past shopping and financing records. Thus, ignoring the modelling of the relation between the credit policies and the credit features may cause a big bias in estimating the action-response causality. The second bias comes from the estimation of predictors-response relation. This is a regression bias related to data and ML algorithm, e.g., the sample size, the feature dimensions and the regressor selections.

The goal of this paper is to construct the estimators of the causal parameters which can address both the confounding bias and the regression bias. It is equivalent to finding the score functions with specific conditions (detailed discussions will be presented in the later sections) such that we can recover the estimators from the score functions. To obtain the corresponding estimates of the estimators, we need to estimate the “counterfactuals”, which are the potential outcomes in delinquent probabilities of borrowers if they were given different amounts of credit lines from the ones they had received. Once the counterfactuals are estimated, we would like to assess both the Average Treatment Effect (ATE) and the Average Treatment Effect on the Treated (ATTE). For new customers, the lender can use the ATE to choose appropriate credit lines for them. For existing customers, the ATTE allows the lender to gauge the potential changes of risks if their credit lines were changed from the current levels to new ones.

For the rest of the paper, Section 2 summarizes the related works and our contributions. Section 3 presents our model setup, and Section 4 presents all the experiments. The paper ends with the conclusion section.

In the past credit risk works, the typical supervised learning methods such as direct regression are widely used to estimate the global relationship between the response and the predictors. For example, the regression tree (Khandani, Kim, and Lo 2010), Random Forests (Malekipirbazari and Aksakalli 2015), and Recurrent Neural Network (Sirignano, Sadhwani, and Giesecke 2016) are applied to construct default forecasting models.

However, when it comes to studying the action-response relationship (e.g., estimating the ATE and ATTE), simply using the typical supervised learning methods to estimate the outcomes for each individual under different interventions and averaging the estimated outcomes for each intervention can produce unsatisfactory results (Chernozhukov et al. 2018), since there is a chance of misspecification of the relationship when confounding effects are present. Some other methods that can account for the confounding effects range from those based on balancing learning and matching (Li and Fu 2017; Kallus 2018; Bennett and Kallus 2019) to those based on deep learning such as representation learning and adversarial learning (Johansson, Shalit, and Sontag 2016; Yao et al. 2018; Lattimore, Lattimore, and Reid 2016; Yoon, Jordon, and van der Schaar 2018). However, for matching methods such as Propensity Score Matching (PSM) and inverse probability weighting (IPW) (e.g., (Hirano, Imbens, and Ridder 2003)), they may amplify the estimation bias if the feature variables are not selected properly or the algorithm is not ideal enough (Heckman, Ichimura, and Todd 1998).

To overcome some deficiencies of the above methods, Doubly Robust Estimators (DREs) was proposed (Farrell 2015). The DREs are recovered from the score functions which incorporate the confounding effects in general (Dudík, Langford, and Li 2011). However, it is not sure if the score functions of the DREs satisfy the orthogonal condition, which is defined in (Chernozhukov et al. 2018) inspired by (Neyman 1979). Heuristically, those DREs recovered from the score functions which may violate the orthogonal condition (see the detailed discussions in our supplementary) can be sensitive to the nuisance parameters, and hence easily lead to a biased estimation. To solve this problem, some researchers propose the new score functions that satisfy the orthogonal condition (Chernozhukov et al. 2018; Mackey, Syrgkanis, and Zadik 2018; Oprescu, Syrgkanis, and Wu 2019), but they either only consider the binary treatment or derive the theoretical results based on the partially linear model (PLR) setting. To improve that, we not only extend the intervention variable from the binary values to the multiple values, but also consider the fully nonlinear model setting instead of the PLR model setting.

The contributions of our paper are:1.We are the first to show the importance and necessity of considering causality in retail credit risk study using observational records of e-commerce lenders and borrows;2.We extend from a partially linear model (e.g., (Mackey, Syrgkanis, and Zadik 2018; Oprescu, Syrgkanis, and Wu 2019)) to a fully nonlinear model. Our estimators recovered from the orthogonal score functions are proved to be regularization unbiased and consistent with an increasing number of population size, thus very suitable for large datasets. Besides, our setup allows the intervention variable to take multiple discrete values, rather than binary values as in (Chernozhukov et al. 2018);3.Our estimators are generic, not only restricted to linear or tree-based methods, but also for any complex methods such as neural network-based models including fully-connected ones (e.g., MLP), convolutional ones (e.g., CNN), and recurrent ones (e.g., GRU);4.The obtained estimators are robust to model misspecifications when mappings between predictors and outcomes/interventions exhibit different degrees of nonlinearity, and data possess different distributional properties than assumed;5.We use the parameters in our experiments to control the causality and nonlinearity and show how much error our estimators can correct for compared with direct regression estimators used in the past credit risk works.The above points are comprehensively tested through well-designed simulation experiments and verified on a large proprietary real-world dataset via semi-synthetic experiments.

Knowing how to estimate the counterfactuals provides a way to estimate two quantities, both of them are the average “action-response” relationship between the potential outcome Y𝑌Y and the potential treatment disuperscript𝑑𝑖d^{i}. Note that θisuperscript𝜃𝑖\theta^{i} is an average over the whole population N𝑁N, whereas θi|jsuperscript𝜃conditional𝑖𝑗\theta^{i|j} is an average over the sub-population Njsubscript𝑁𝑗N_{j}. To compute the estimates of the two quantities, the key point is to obtain g^^𝑔\hat{g} (the estimate of g𝑔g) for every disuperscript𝑑𝑖d^{i} from the observational dataset. In the related literature, specifications of g𝑔g include both the additive forms (Djebbari and Smith 2008) and the multiplicative forms (Hainmueller, Mummolo, and Xu 2019). The choices of g^^𝑔\hat{g} also include linear models (Du and Zhang 2015; Li and Bell 2017) as well as nonlinear ones. In particular, the predictive advantage of using neural networks to estimate g𝑔g is demonstrated in (Shi, Blei, and Veitch 2019) where they formulated a similar relationship to estimate the ATE and the ATTE (Louizos et al. 2017; Yoon, Jordon, and van der Schaar 2018). Other examples can be found in (Alaa and Schaar 2018; Toulis and Parkes 2016; Li and Fu 2017; Syrgkanis et al. 2019).

Once we obtain g^​(di,⋅)^𝑔superscript𝑑𝑖⋅\hat{g}(d^{i},\cdot) (the estimate of g​(di,⋅)𝑔superscript𝑑𝑖⋅g(d^{i},\cdot)), θisuperscript𝜃𝑖\theta^{i} and θi∣jsuperscript𝜃conditional𝑖𝑗\theta^{i\mid j} can be respectively estimated in the IoC context asθ^oi=1N​∑m=1𝑁​g^​(di,𝐳m)​and​θ^oi∣j=1Nj​∑m=1Nj​g^​(di,𝐳mj).superscriptsubscript^𝜃𝑜𝑖1𝑁𝑁𝑚1^𝑔superscript𝑑𝑖subscript𝐳𝑚andsuperscriptsubscript^𝜃𝑜conditional𝑖𝑗1subscript𝑁𝑗subscript𝑁𝑗𝑚1^𝑔superscript𝑑𝑖superscriptsubscript𝐳𝑚𝑗\displaystyle\scalebox{1.0}{$\hat{\theta}_{o}^{i}=\frac{1}{N}\overset{N}{\underset{m=1}{\sum}}\hat{g}(d^{i},\mathbf{z}_{m})\;\;\;\text{and}\;\;\;\hat{\theta}_{o}^{i\mid j}=\frac{1}{N_{j}}\overset{N_{j}}{\underset{m=1}{\sum}}\hat{g}(d^{i},\mathbf{z}_{m}^{j})$}.We call them the IoC estimates since we omit the relationship between D𝐷D and 𝐙𝐙\mathbf{Z}, or the so-called confounding effects. Indeed, the confounding effects are obscured from the IoC estimates.

We then propose the Impact Inference with Confounding (IwC) which can account for both the policy impact and the confounding effects.Using the IoC estimates to estimate θisuperscript𝜃𝑖\theta^{i} and θi|jsuperscript𝜃conditional𝑖𝑗\theta^{i|j} can be misspecified (Dudík, Langford, and Li 2011; Yuan et al. 2019) when the confounding effects are present.The misspecification comes from the fact that, while 𝐙𝐙\mathbf{Z} in (1) affects the outcome Y𝑌Y, the intervention D𝐷D could also be driven by the confounding variable 𝐙𝐙\mathbf{Z}. For example, the customer’s income level could be a confounding variable in the retail lending context. Customers who receive higher credit lines usually have higher incomes, and higher-income people tend to have lower credit risk. Such examples also widely exist in recommendation systems (e.g., (Wang et al. 2019; Swaminathan andJoachims 2015a; Swaminathan and Joachims 2015b) and references therein). In our paper, we construct better score functions satisfying the orthogonal condition such that the corresponding estimates are assured to be regularization unbiased (Chernozhukov et al. 2018).

In order to study the policy impact in the presence of confounding effects, we propose the following formulation for our IwC estimations:Y𝑌\displaystyle Y=g​(D,𝐔,𝐙)+ξ,𝔼​[ξ∣𝐔,𝐗,𝐙]=0,formulae-sequenceabsent𝑔𝐷𝐔𝐙𝜉𝔼delimited-[]conditional𝜉𝐔𝐗𝐙0\displaystyle=g(D,\mathbf{U},\mathbf{Z})+\xi,\quad\mathbb{E}\left[\xi\mid\mathbf{U},\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Z}\right]=0,(2a)D𝐷\displaystyle D=m​(𝐗,𝐙,ν),𝔼​[ν∣𝐗,𝐙]=0,formulae-sequenceabsent𝑚𝐗𝐙𝜈𝔼delimited-[]conditional𝜈𝐗𝐙0\displaystyle=m(\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Z},\nu),\quad\quad\mathbb{E}\left[\nu\mid\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Z}\right]=0,(2b)where 𝐙𝐙\mathbf{Z} is the confounder variable, 𝐔𝐔\mathbf{U} is the outcome-specific feature set, and 𝐗𝐗\mathbf{X} is the intervention-specific feature set. The map m𝑚m and the noise term ν𝜈\nu are of the same nature as those of g𝑔g and ξ𝜉\xi. We impose no functional restrictions on g𝑔g and m𝑚m such that they can be parametric or non-parametric, linear or nonlinear, etc.

If the confounding effects (2b) are not recognized but present in the data, the IoC estimatesθ^oi=1N​∑m=1𝑁​g^​(di,𝐮m,𝐳m),θ^oi∣j=1Nj​∑m=1Nj​g^​(di,𝐮mj,𝐳mj)formulae-sequencesuperscriptsubscript^𝜃𝑜𝑖1𝑁𝑁𝑚1^𝑔superscript𝑑𝑖subscript𝐮𝑚subscript𝐳𝑚superscriptsubscript^𝜃𝑜conditional𝑖𝑗1subscript𝑁𝑗subscript𝑁𝑗𝑚1^𝑔superscript𝑑𝑖superscriptsubscript𝐮𝑚𝑗superscriptsubscript𝐳𝑚𝑗\displaystyle\hat{\theta}_{o}^{i}=\frac{1}{N}\overset{N}{\underset{m=1}{\sum}}\hat{g}(d^{i},\mathbf{u}_{m},\mathbf{z}_{m}),\;\hat{\theta}_{o}^{i\mid j}=\frac{1}{N_{j}}\overset{N_{j}}{\underset{m=1}{\sum}}\hat{g}(d^{i},\mathbf{u}_{m}^{j},\mathbf{z}_{m}^{j})(3)could be inaccurate. Indeed, we ignore the impacts caused by (2b) when (3) are used. Thus, the estimated outcome-predictors relation g^^𝑔\hat{g} can have opposite outcome-predictors relation of the authentic g𝑔g w.r.t. the features variable. Furthermore, even if g^^𝑔\hat{g} has the similar relation with g𝑔g w.r.t. the features variable, the (3) can be regularized biased, meaning that the estimators are sensitive to the estimation g^^𝑔\hat{g}.As such, we should construct the estimators which use the information given in (2b) and regularized unbiased. PSM, IPW and doubly robust (DRE) approach (e.g., (Farrell 2015)) are methodologies which incorporate the relation (2b) through the computation of propensity score. However, the DREs can be sensitive w.r.t. the small perturbations on the map m𝑚m in (2b). Consequently, it may not be suitable for the estimations of ATE and ATTE. To stabilize it, we should build the estimators which can be recovered from the score functions that satisfy the orthogonal condition in the Definition 3.1. Heuristically, the partial derivative of score functions w.r.t. the nuisance parameters are expected to be 00. Indeed, the regularization biases of the estimators ATE and ATTE are reduced using a multiplicative term of the propensity score and the residuals between the observed Y𝑌Y and the estimate of g​(di,⋅,⋅)𝑔superscript𝑑𝑖⋅⋅g(d^{i},\cdot,\cdot).

Our aim is to construct the score functions ψ𝜓\psi such that the moments of the Gateaux derivative of ψ𝜓\psi w.r.t. ℊ​(di,𝐔,𝐙)ℊsuperscript𝑑𝑖𝐔𝐙\mathcal{g}(d^{i},\mathbf{U},\mathbf{Z}), ai​(𝐗,𝐙)subscript𝑎𝑖𝐗𝐙a_{i}(\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Z}) and mjsubscript𝑚𝑗m_{j} evaluating at g​(di,𝐔,𝐙)𝑔superscript𝑑𝑖𝐔𝐙g(d^{i},\mathbf{U},\mathbf{Z}), 𝔼​[𝟏{D=di}∣𝐗,𝐙]𝔼delimited-[]conditionalsubscript1𝐷superscript𝑑𝑖𝐗𝐙\mathbb{E}\left[\mathbf{1}_{\{D=d^{i}\}}\mid\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Z}\right] and 𝔼​[𝟏{D=dj}]𝔼delimited-[]subscript1𝐷superscript𝑑𝑗\mathbb{E}\left[\mathbf{1}_{\{D=d^{j}\}}\right] are 00, implying the Definition 3.1 holds.

Before stating the score functions, we introduce some notations to simplify our expression. We define the estimate of g​(di,𝐱,𝐳)𝑔superscript𝑑𝑖𝐱𝐳g(d^{i},\mathbf{x},\mathbf{z}) as g^​(di,𝐱,𝐳)^𝑔superscript𝑑𝑖𝐱𝐳\hat{g}(d^{i},\mathbf{x},\mathbf{z}). Furthermore, we define Pi​(𝐱,𝐳)=𝔼​[𝟏{D=di}∣𝐗=𝐱,𝐙=𝐳]subscript𝑃𝑖𝐱𝐳𝔼delimited-[]formulae-sequenceconditionalsubscript1𝐷superscript𝑑𝑖𝐗𝐱𝐙𝐳P_{i}(\mathbf{x},\mathbf{z})=\mathbb{E}[\mathbf{1}_{\{D=d^{i}\}}\mid\mathbf{X}=\mathbf{x},\mathbf{Z}=\mathbf{z}] and the corresponding estimate as P^i​(𝐱,𝐳)subscript^𝑃𝑖𝐱𝐳\hat{P}_{i}(\mathbf{x},\mathbf{z}) for any i=1,⋯,n𝑖1⋯𝑛i=1,\cdots,n. To find P^i​(𝐱,𝐳)subscript^𝑃𝑖𝐱𝐳\hat{P}_{i}(\mathbf{x},\mathbf{z}), we can use any classification methods to obtain it. For example, when we use Logistic regression to estimate P^i​(𝐱,𝐳)subscript^𝑃𝑖𝐱𝐳\hat{P}_{i}(\mathbf{x},\mathbf{z}), it becomes 1/[1+exp⁡(−𝐰xT​𝐱−𝐰zT​𝐳−𝐰)]1delimited-[]1superscriptsubscript𝐰𝑥𝑇𝐱superscriptsubscript𝐰𝑧𝑇𝐳𝐰1/\big{[}1+\exp\left(-\mathbf{w}_{x}^{T}\mathbf{x}-\mathbf{w}_{z}^{T}\mathbf{z}-\mathbf{w}\right)\big{]}.

We defer the detailed derivations in the supplementary.Heuristically, we can recover the estimates of θisuperscript𝜃𝑖\theta^{i} and θi∣jsuperscript𝜃conditional𝑖𝑗\theta^{i\mid j} (denoted as θ^wisuperscriptsubscript^𝜃𝑤𝑖\hat{\theta}_{w}^{i} and θ^wi∣jsuperscriptsubscript^𝜃𝑤conditional𝑖𝑗\hat{\theta}_{w}^{i\mid j}) from 𝔼​[ψi​(W,ϑ,ϱ)∣ϑ=θ,ϱ=ρ]=0𝔼delimited-[]evaluated-atsuperscript𝜓𝑖𝑊italic-ϑitalic-ϱformulae-sequenceitalic-ϑ𝜃italic-ϱ𝜌0\mathbb{E}\left[\psi^{i}(W,\vartheta,\varrho)\mid_{\vartheta=\theta,\varrho=\rho}\right]=0 and 𝔼​[ψi∣j​(W,ϑ,ϱ)∣ϑ=θ,ϱ=ρ]=0𝔼delimited-[]evaluated-atsuperscript𝜓conditional𝑖𝑗𝑊italic-ϑitalic-ϱformulae-sequenceitalic-ϑ𝜃italic-ϱ𝜌0\mathbb{E}\left[\psi^{i\mid j}(W,\vartheta,\varrho)\mid_{\vartheta=\theta,\varrho=\rho}\right]=0 respectively, which are:θ^wisuperscriptsubscript^𝜃𝑤𝑖\displaystyle\hat{\theta}_{w}^{i}=1N{∑m=1𝑁g^(di,𝐮m,𝐳m)\displaystyle=\frac{1}{N}\left\{\overset{N}{\underset{m=1}{\sum}}\hat{g}(d^{i},\mathbf{u}_{m},\mathbf{z}_{m})\right.+∑m=1Ni(ymi−g^​(di,𝐮mi,𝐳mi))P^i​(𝐱mi,𝐳mi)},\displaystyle\qquad\left.+\overset{N_{i}}{\underset{m=1}{\sum}}\frac{(y_{m}^{i}-\hat{g}(d^{i},\mathbf{u}_{m}^{i},\mathbf{z}_{m}^{i}))}{\hat{P}_{i}(\mathbf{x}_{m}^{i},\mathbf{z}_{m}^{i})}\right\},(7)θ^wi∣jsuperscriptsubscript^𝜃𝑤conditional𝑖𝑗\displaystyle\hat{\theta}_{w}^{i\mid j}=1Nj{∑m=1Njg^(di,𝐮mj,𝐳mj)\displaystyle=\frac{1}{N_{j}}\left\{\overset{N_{j}}{\underset{m=1}{\sum}}\hat{g}(d^{i},\mathbf{u}_{m}^{j},\mathbf{z}_{m}^{j})\right.+∑m=1NiP^j​(𝐱mi,𝐳mi)P^i​(𝐱mi,𝐳mi)[ymi−g^(di,𝐮mi,𝐳mi)]}.\displaystyle\;\left.+\overset{N_{i}}{\underset{m=1}{\sum}}\frac{\hat{P}_{j}(\mathbf{x}_{m}^{i},\mathbf{z}_{m}^{i})}{\hat{P}_{i}(\mathbf{x}_{m}^{i},\mathbf{z}_{m}^{i})}\left[y_{m}^{i}-\hat{g}(d^{i},\mathbf{u}_{m}^{i},\mathbf{z}_{m}^{i})\right]\right\}.(8)

We call θ^wisuperscriptsubscript^𝜃𝑤𝑖\hat{\theta}_{w}^{i} and θ^wi∣jsuperscriptsubscript^𝜃𝑤conditional𝑖𝑗\hat{\theta}_{w}^{i\mid j} in (7) and (8) the IwC estimates. They are regularization unbiased when the residuals between the observed Y𝑌Y and the estimate of g​(di,⋅,⋅)𝑔superscript𝑑𝑖⋅⋅g(d^{i},\cdot,\cdot) are used as the regularization term. Besides, they are the consistent estimates (see the Remark 3.3). Theoritically, we can study the consistency using error decomposition (see in the supplementary). We also study the consistency with numerical results in the Section 4.

Whenever the estimates of θisuperscript𝜃𝑖\theta^{i} and θi|jsuperscript𝜃conditional𝑖𝑗\theta^{i|j}, i.e., θ^isuperscript^𝜃𝑖\hat{\theta}^{i} and θ^i|jsuperscript^𝜃conditional𝑖𝑗\hat{\theta}^{i|j} are available, we can estimate the Average Treatment Effect (ATE) and the Average Treatment Effect on the Treated (ATTE) asATE^​(i,k)^ATE𝑖𝑘\displaystyle\hat{\textrm{ATE}}(i,k):=θ^i,k=θ^i−θ^kassignabsentsuperscript^𝜃𝑖𝑘superscript^𝜃𝑖superscript^𝜃𝑘\displaystyle:=\hat{\theta}^{i,k}=\hat{\theta}^{i}-\hat{\theta}^{k}(9)ATTE^​(i,k|j)^ATTE𝑖conditional𝑘𝑗\displaystyle\hat{\textrm{ATTE}}(i,k|j):=θ^i,k∣j=θ^i∣j−θ^k∣j.assignabsentsuperscript^𝜃𝑖conditional𝑘𝑗superscript^𝜃conditional𝑖𝑗superscript^𝜃conditional𝑘𝑗\displaystyle:=\hat{\theta}^{i,k\mid j}=\hat{\theta}^{i\mid j}-\hat{\theta}^{k\mid j}.For IoC formulation, the estimates of ATE and ATTE are denoted as θ^oi,ksuperscriptsubscript^𝜃𝑜𝑖𝑘\hat{\theta}_{o}^{i,k} and θ^oi,k∣jsuperscriptsubscript^𝜃𝑜𝑖conditional𝑘𝑗\hat{\theta}_{o}^{i,k\mid j} which can be computed using (3). For IwC formulation, they are θ^wi,ksuperscriptsubscript^𝜃𝑤𝑖𝑘\hat{\theta}_{w}^{i,k} and θ^wi,k∣jsuperscriptsubscript^𝜃𝑤𝑖conditional𝑘𝑗\hat{\theta}_{w}^{i,k\mid j} computed by (​7​)italic-(7italic-)\eqref{eqt:desired unbiased estimator expectation} and (​8​)italic-(8italic-)\eqref{eqt:desired unbiased estimator conditional expectation} respectively.

We now set out experiments to i) estimate the counterfactuals and the treatment effects under different settings and ii) assess the consistency and robustness properties under IwC formulation.

Our comparisons are made across two aspects: 1) different data properties per (10a) & (10d), and 2) different choices of the map g^^𝑔\hat{g} per (2a). For 1), we generate simulated datasets that possess three main properties: a) different levels of causal effect the intervention D𝐷Dcauses on the outcome Y𝑌Y, b) different degrees of nonlinearity of this causal impact, and c) different tail heaviness in the distribution of the feature set (𝐗,𝐔,𝐙)𝐗𝐔𝐙(\mathbf{X,U,Z}). For 2), the various maps under testing include three most commonly used neural network nonlinear models: the Multi-layer Perception Network (MLP), the Convolutional Neural Network (CNN), and the Gated Recurrent Unit (GRU); we also contrast them with widely recognized classic models such as the ordinary least square (OLS) and OLS with LASSO and RIDGE regularization, and the decision-tree based models such as the Random Forest (RF) and one with boosting features, the xgboost (XGB).

For every set of simulated data and a given choice of g𝑔g, we report estimations of ATE & ATTE per (9), using both our IwC estimations(7) (8) and the IoC estimations (3). All results are out-of-sample and we use 70%percent7070\% of data as the training set and the remaining 30%percent3030\% as the testing set. We use grid search to find the optimal hyperparameters of the linear models and the tree-based models. For all neural network-based models, we use the Bayesian optimization to find the optimal hyperparameters. The number of hidden layers ranges from 222 to 777 and the number of units for each layer from 505050 to 500500500. The batch size is in integer multiples of 323232 and optimized within [32,3200]323200[32,3200]. We search the learning rate between 0.00010.00010.0001 and 0.10.10.1. The experiments are run on two Ubuntu HP Z4 Workstations each with Intel Core i9 10-Core CPU at 3.3GHz, 128G DIMM-2166 ECC RAM, and two sets of NVIDIA Quadro RTX 5000 GPU. The total computation time of Table 1 and Table 3 is 177 hours, including all different sets of α𝛼\alpha and β𝛽\beta, with each set containing 888 models; Figure 1 and Figure 2 cost 163 hours in total.The Data Generating Process. As the ground truth (the factuals and counterfactuals) is unavailable, we construct a data generating process (DGP) for our credit-related dataset similar to many causal learning function σ𝜎\sigma maps the features to the intervention variable D𝐷D such that D𝐷D takes five treatment levels The confounding features set is a 202020-dimensional random vector 𝐙=[Z1,⋯,Z20]T𝐙superscriptsubscript𝑍1⋯subscript𝑍20𝑇\mathbf{Z}=[Z_{1},\cdots,Z_{20}]^{T}. The parameter values of a,b𝑎𝑏a,b used to generate the correlation matrix, (λ,γ,b1,b2)𝜆𝛾subscript𝑏1subscript𝑏2(\lambda,\gamma,b_{1},b_{2}) in (10c), (𝐚0,τ,r,e1,e2)subscript𝐚0𝜏𝑟subscript𝑒1subscript𝑒2(\mathbf{a}_{0},\tau,r,e_{1},e_{2}) in (10b) , (𝐚1,𝐚2)subscript𝐚1subscript𝐚2(\mathbf{a}_{1},\mathbf{a}_{2}) in (10c), and ({𝐜iz}i=14,{𝐜iu}i=14)superscriptsubscriptsuperscriptsubscript𝐜𝑖𝑧𝑖14superscriptsubscriptsuperscriptsubscript𝐜𝑖𝑢𝑖14(\left\{\mathbf{c}_{i}^{z}\right\}_{i=1}^{4},\left\{\mathbf{c}_{i}^{u}\right\}_{i=1}^{4}) in (11) are deferred in the supplementary due to space constraints.The quantity 𝐙¯rsubscript¯𝐙𝑟\bar{\mathbf{Z}}_{r} (or 𝐔¯rsubscript¯𝐔𝑟\bar{\mathbf{U}}_{r}) in (11) is a column vector such that each entry is the product of r𝑟r elements taken from 𝐙𝐙\mathbf{Z} (or 𝐔𝐔\mathbf{U}) without repetition. For example, 𝐙¯2=[Z1​Z2,Z1​Z3,⋯,Z19​Z20]Tsubscript¯𝐙2superscriptsubscript𝑍1subscript𝑍2subscript𝑍1subscript𝑍3⋯subscript𝑍19subscript𝑍20𝑇\bar{\mathbf{Z}}_{2}=[Z_{1}Z_{2},Z_{1}Z_{3},\cdots,Z_{19}Z_{20}]^{T}, 𝐙¯3=[Z1​Z2​Z3,Z1​Z2​Z4,⋯,Z18​Z19​Z20]Tsubscript¯𝐙3superscriptsubscript𝑍1subscript𝑍2subscript𝑍3subscript𝑍1subscript𝑍2subscript𝑍4⋯subscript𝑍18subscript𝑍19subscript𝑍20𝑇\bar{\mathbf{Z}}_{3}=[Z_{1}Z_{2}Z_{3},Z_{1}Z_{2}Z_{4},\cdots,Z_{18}Z_{19}Z_{20}]^{T}, etc.

Our DGP is reasonable for the following reasons:1) In many causal works, the researchers never check if their DGP can appropriately fit the real-world data given in their papers ((Hainmueller, Mummolo, and Xu 2019; Hill 2011; Lim 2018)). Our DGP fits the highly nonlinear and correlated real-world credit data very well. For example, when we fit the real-world dataset (the one used in our semi-synthetic experiment) to (10c), the relative mean square error (MSE) we obtained is 7.9% which is very small. 2) Our DGP is generic enough. The functions in our DGP, such as the power/exponential/logarithm/inverse, are all out of lengthy testings of each individual’s feature separately. 3) It allows us to control the degrees of causality and nonlinearity. First, the parameter α∈[0,1]𝛼01\alpha\in[0,1] in (10d) controls the amount of the policy impact. The bigger α𝛼\alpha is, the smaller the impact. In the case α=1𝛼1\alpha=1, Y𝑌Y is no longer a function of the intervention. Second, the parameters β∈[0,1]𝛽01\beta\in[0,1] and n,m∈ℝ+𝑛𝑚superscriptℝn,m\in\mathbb{R}^{+} control the degree of nonlinearity for a fixed α𝛼\alpha. For instance, when m=1,n=2formulae-sequence𝑚1𝑛2m=1,n=2, the smaller β𝛽\beta is, the larger the contribution from the term exp⁡(Dn)superscript𝐷𝑛\exp(D^{n}), hence the more degree of nonlinearity.Causality vs. Nonlinearity. Table 1 compares the ATE estimations comprehensively across different choices of g^^𝑔\hat{g} and different data properties per (10a) & (10d). Each number in column a) “IoC” and column b) “IwC” is a weighted average of the ATE estimation in relative errors w.r.t the true ATE, computed from 40,0004000040,000 observations and 100100100 independent experiments for each individual setting:1M​∑m=1𝑀​∑1≤i,k≤ni≠k​[|θi,k;m|∑|1≤i,k≤ni≠kθi,k;m|​|θ^i,k;mθi,k;m−1|],\displaystyle\frac{1}{M}\overset{M}{\underset{m=1}{\sum}}\;\underset{\underset{i\neq k}{1\leq i,k\leq n}}{\sum}\left[\frac{\left|\theta^{i,k;m}\right|}{\sum{{}_{\underset{i\neq k}{1\leq i,k\leq n}}}\left|\theta^{i,k;m}\right|}\left|\frac{\hat{\theta}^{i,k;m}}{\theta^{i,k;m}}-1\right|\right],(12)where M𝑀M is the number of experiments conducted, θi,k;msuperscript𝜃𝑖𝑘𝑚\theta^{i,k;m} is the true value of θi,ksuperscript𝜃𝑖𝑘\theta^{i,k} in the mthsuperscript𝑚thm^{\textrm{th}} experiment, and θ^i,k;msuperscript^𝜃𝑖𝑘𝑚\hat{\theta}^{i,k;m} is the estimate of θi,ksuperscript𝜃𝑖𝑘\theta^{i,k} in the mthsuperscript𝑚thm^{\textrm{th}} experiment based on IwC (or IoC).The ATTE cases are deferred in the supplementary.

Table 1 reports the results when the α𝛼\alpha and β𝛽\beta in (10d) are fixed at different values. When we fix α=5%𝛼percent5\alpha=5\%, the amount of impact of the intervention D𝐷D on the outcome Y𝑌Y is large. We call it the “strong causality” case. When the causal effects are strong, whether the data are light-tail distributed or heavy-tail distributed, mostly linear (β=95%𝛽percent95\beta=95\%) or mostly nonlinear (β=5%𝛽percent5\beta=5\%), and whether the choice of g^^𝑔\hat{g} is a linear model (OLS, LASSO, RIDGE), a tree-based model (RF, XGB) or a neural-network-based model (GRU, CNN, MLP), the IwC estimates always give superior ATE estimations. Similar observations can be made in the “strong nonlinearity” case in Table 1. In this case, we fix β=5%𝛽percent5\beta=5\%, meaning that the impact of the intervention D𝐷D on the outcome Y𝑌Y is very nonlinear, irrespective of whether the amount of the impact is larger or smaller.

Even if one uses misspecified linear models such as the OLS, LASSO, and RIDGE on heavy-tail distributed highly-nonlinear data, there is at least a 30%percent3030\% reduction in the estimation errors. When the causal effects and nonlinearity are both strong in the data and one did use the right nonlinear models, e.g., RF, XGB, GRU, CNN, MLP, the error can be reduced by at least 82.7%percent82.782.7\% for the light-tail data and 75.8%percent75.875.8\% for the heavy-tail data. The significant superiority of IwC vs. IoC ATE estimation across all our settings suggests the importance of considering “action-response” relationship.Consistency of the Estimators.  We demonstrate the consistency of the estimators through numerical experiments. We repeat the simulated experiment for 100100100 times, then compute two quantities. The first quantity requires us to compute the values of θisuperscript𝜃𝑖\theta^{i} and θ^wisuperscriptsubscript^𝜃𝑤𝑖\hat{\theta}_{w}^{i} based on 100100100 experiments and find the corresponding relative errors for each i𝑖i. We then find the mean of all the relative errors. The second quantity is the average of the standard deviation of the difference between θisuperscript𝜃𝑖\theta^{i} and θ^wisuperscriptsubscript^𝜃𝑤𝑖\hat{\theta}_{w}^{i} of 100100100 experiments. Indeed, the first quantity is computed as (13a) and the second quantity is computed as (13b) which are stated as follows:1K​∑i=1𝐾​|∑m=1𝑀​θ^wi;m∑m=1𝑀​θi;m−1|1𝐾𝐾𝑖1𝑀𝑚1superscriptsubscript^𝜃𝑤𝑖𝑚𝑀𝑚1superscript𝜃𝑖𝑚1\displaystyle\scalebox{0.88}{$\frac{1}{K}\overset{K}{\underset{i=1}{\sum}}\left|\frac{\overset{M}{\underset{m=1}{\sum}}{\hat{\theta}}_{w}^{i;m}}{\overset{M}{\underset{m=1}{\sum}}\theta^{i;m}}-1\right|$}(13a)1K​∑i=1𝐾​1M−1​∑m=1𝑀​([θ^wi;m−θi;m]−1M​∑s=1𝑀​[θ^wi;s−θi;s])2.1𝐾𝐾𝑖11𝑀1𝑀𝑚1superscriptdelimited-[]superscriptsubscript^𝜃𝑤𝑖𝑚superscript𝜃𝑖𝑚1𝑀𝑀𝑠1delimited-[]superscriptsubscript^𝜃𝑤𝑖𝑠superscript𝜃𝑖𝑠2\displaystyle\scalebox{0.85}{$\frac{1}{K}\overset{K}{\underset{i=1}{\sum}}\sqrt{\frac{1}{M-1}\overset{M}{\underset{m=1}{\sum}}\left([\hat{\theta}_{w}^{i;m}-\theta^{i;m}]-\frac{1}{M}\overset{M}{\underset{s=1}{\sum}}[\hat{\theta}_{w}^{i;s}-\theta^{i;s}]\right)^{2}}$}.(13b)Here K𝐾K and M𝑀M in (13a) and (13b) are the number of estimators and the number of experiments respectively.

To show that the estimators are consistent, we compare the mean of all the relative errors versus the number of observational data and summarize the results in Figure 1.We notice that when the number of observational data increases, the computed values in each plot becomes smaller except using linear regressors in computing the mean relative error between θisuperscript𝜃𝑖\theta^{i} and θ^wisuperscriptsubscript^𝜃𝑤𝑖\hat{\theta}_{w}^{i}. It implies that using linear regressors would cause biased estimations but not the case of using nonlinear regressors. This matches with our expectations since the generated dataset is nonlinear. The consistency analysis of θ^wi∣jsuperscriptsubscript^𝜃𝑤conditional𝑖𝑗\hat{\theta}_{w}^{i\mid j} is deferred in the supplementary.

Testing on Real-world Data.We now apply our method to a unique real-world anonymous dataset kindly provided by one of the largest global technology firms that operates in both the e-commerce business and the lending business. The dataset contains observational records of 400,000400000400,000 concurrent customers as of writing this paper. The feature dimension of the raw data is 115911591159, including 1) the borrowers’ shopping, purchasing and payment histories; 2) credit lines, interest charges and financing application decisions set by the lender’s decision algorithm; 3) outstanding amounts and delinquency records. After autoencoding, the dimension of the feature set is reduced to 404040, which will be our (𝐙,𝐔,𝐗)𝐙𝐔𝐗(\mathbf{Z,U,X}). Descriptive statistics of a few representative and important features are stated in Table 2.

In the field of credit risk analysis, a default outcome is defined w.r.t. a specific credit event.The event in this study is the “three-month delinquent” event. It is the borrowers’ payment for any of their outstanding loans within the next three months, no matter the borrowers are late on the payment or not.The intervention variable is the credit line set by the lender’s lending algorithm, at the time the customer apply for shopping credit to finance their online purchases.

Different from simulation studies, the true θisuperscript𝜃𝑖\theta^{i} and θi∣jsuperscript𝜃conditional𝑖𝑗\theta^{i\mid j} are unavailable since the counterfactuals can never be observed in the real application. As such, we adopt the semi-synthetic approach to generate the ground truth of counterfactuals, as commonly used in the literature (e.g., (Hill 2011; Johansson, Shalit, and Sontag 2016; Louizos et al. 2017) and references therein). The details of the semi-synthetic setup can be found in the supplementary.

The results reported in Figure 2 and Table 3 are strikingly encouraging, given these are from the real-world data. Not only do we see a single-digit percentage estimation error in all settings whenever the IwC estimates are used, we see both significant and robust error reductions compared to the IoC estimates.

This paper presents the first retail credit risk study that estimates the action-response causality from observational data of both a) the e-commerce lender’s credit decision records and b) their borrowers’ purchase, borrowing, and payment records. Our study shows that the confounding effects between the lender’s credit decisions and the borrowers’ credit risks, if overlooked, would result in significant biases in risk assessment. Using our IwC formulations, the biases can be reduced to a few percentages. The larger the dataset is, the higher the error reduction. The nature of the current study is about state estimation. Our future study will be towards the decision making problem built on the current framework.

In the section “Details of models for simulated data”, we give the detailed descriptions about the models which are used for simulated data generating process and semi-synthetic data generating process. In the section “Tables and Figures”, we summarize all the tables and figures which are not included in the main paper. In the section “Proofs of Theorem”, we provide detailed proofs of Theorems in the main paper. In the section “Score functions of IoC and Doubly Robust (DR) estimators”, we state out the score functions which can be used to recover the IoC and doubly robust (DR) estimators, and check if the corresponding score functions satisfy the moment condition and the orthogonal condition. In the section “Consistency”, we undergo error decomposition between the ‘true’ quantity and the corresponding IwC estimator, which allows us to study the consistency of the IwC estimators.

In this section, we provide detailed descriptions about the notations which appear in the main paper and the Appendix.

A Tangible Example We now work through an example to illustrate the difference between the estimations from causal impact inference and the estimations from non-causal impact inference. In this example, the outcome Y𝑌Y is the delinquency probability of an customer indicating how likely he will be late for the next payment. The intervention D𝐷D is the credit limit set by the e-commerce lender. The common factor 𝐙𝐙\mathbf{Z} that confounds both Y𝑌Y and D𝐷D is the monthly income. The intervention-specific variable 𝐗𝐗\mathbf{X} is his age, and the outcome-specific variable 𝐔𝐔\mathbf{U} is his monthly expenditure. Generally, when the individual’s income 𝐙𝐙\mathbf{Z} is high and the expenditure 𝐔𝐔\mathbf{U} is low, he is less likely to be late for payment. Meanwhile, the lender tends to increase the credit lines D𝐷D as customers’ higher income 𝐙𝐙\mathbf{Z} grow and decreases their credit lines as they age.Suppose there are two types of credit lines D=$1000𝐷currency-dollar1000D=\$1000 and D=$2000𝐷currency-dollar2000D=\$2000. Let’s assume the relationship between Y𝑌Y and (D,𝐔,𝐙)𝐷𝐔𝐙(D,\mathbf{U},\mathbf{Z}) as well as the relationship between D𝐷D and (𝐗,𝐙)𝐗𝐙(\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Z}) areY​(𝐔,𝐙;D)=g​(D,𝐔,𝐙)+ξ, where g​(D,𝐔,𝐙)={−𝐙+𝐔+10000100000,D=1000−𝐙+𝐔+60000100000,D=2000 and D={1000,𝐙−10​𝐗5000<12000,𝐙−10​𝐗5000≥1​ respectively.\begin{gathered}Y(\mathbf{U},\mathbf{Z};D)=g(D,\mathbf{U},\mathbf{Z})+\xi,\quad\text{ where }\\g(D,\mathbf{U},\mathbf{Z})=\begin{cases}\frac{\mathbf{-Z}+\mathbf{U}+10000}{100000},&\;D=1000\\\frac{\mathbf{-Z}+\mathbf{U}+60000}{100000},&\;D=2000\end{cases}\quad\text{ and }\quad D=\begin{cases}1000,&\;\frac{\mathbf{Z}-10\mathbf{X}}{5000}<1\\2000,&\;\frac{\mathbf{Z}-10\mathbf{X}}{5000}\geq 1\end{cases}\text{\quad respectively.}\end{gathered}(14)Here, ξ𝜉\xi is the normal distributed noise such that it follows N​(0,0.001)𝑁00.001N(0,0.001). In the main paper, we give out the explicit form of q​(𝐔,𝐙)𝑞𝐔𝐙q(\mathbf{U},\mathbf{Z}) and the D𝐷D construction, which areq​(𝐔,𝐙)𝑞𝐔𝐙\displaystyle q(\mathbf{U},\mathbf{Z})={e1​log⁡(e2+k​(𝐙)2+|k​(𝐔)|τ)+exp⁡(|𝐚0T​𝐙|)}r,absentsuperscriptsubscript𝑒1subscript𝑒2𝑘superscript𝐙2superscript𝑘𝐔𝜏superscriptsubscript𝐚0𝑇𝐙𝑟\displaystyle=\left\{e_{1}\log\left(e_{2}+k(\mathbf{Z})^{2}+|k(\mathbf{U})|^{\tau}\right)+\exp\left(\left|\mathbf{a}_{0}^{T}\mathbf{Z}\right|\right)\right\}^{r},D𝐷\displaystyle D=σ​(n​(𝐗,𝐙,ν))=σ​(λ​(𝐚1T​𝐗+|𝐚2T​𝐙|γ+b1​X91+|X3|+b2​X101+|X6|)+ν).absent𝜎𝑛𝐗𝐙𝜈𝜎𝜆superscriptsubscript𝐚1𝑇𝐗superscriptsuperscriptsubscript𝐚2𝑇𝐙𝛾subscript𝑏1subscript𝑋91subscript𝑋3subscript𝑏2subscript𝑋101subscript𝑋6𝜈\displaystyle=\sigma\left(n(\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Z},\nu)\right)=\sigma\left(\lambda\left(\mathbf{a}_{1}^{T}\mathbf{X}+\left|\mathbf{a}_{2}^{T}\mathbf{Z}\right|^{\gamma}+\frac{b_{1}\;X_{9}}{1+|X_{3}|}+\frac{b_{2}\;X_{10}}{1+|X_{6}|}\right)+\nu\right).These forms are chosen for the simulated experiment and the empirical experiment. Differences come from the choices of k​(𝐙)𝑘𝐙k(\mathbf{Z}) and k​(𝐔)𝑘𝐔k(\mathbf{U}). In this section, we give out the numerical values of the coefficients which appear in the terms k​(𝐙)𝑘𝐙k(\mathbf{Z}) and k​(𝐔)𝑘𝐔k(\mathbf{U}). Besides, we determine the credit limit D𝐷D from the function n​(𝐗,𝐙,ν)𝑛𝐗𝐙𝜈n(\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Z},\nu). To assign the credit limit of each individual in a population, we need to divide the whole population into 5 categories based on the values of n​(𝐗,𝐙,ν)𝑛𝐗𝐙𝜈n(\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Z},\nu) of the whole population: 1) <=20absent20<=20 percentile of n​(𝐗,𝐙,ν)𝑛𝐗𝐙𝜈n(\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Z},\nu) of the whole population, 2) in between 20 percentile and 40 percentile of n​(𝐗,𝐙,ν)𝑛𝐗𝐙𝜈n(\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Z},\nu) of the whole population, 3) in between 40 percentile and 60 percentile of n​(𝐗,𝐙,ν)𝑛𝐗𝐙𝜈n(\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Z},\nu) of the whole population, 4) in between 60 and 80 percentile of n​(𝐗,𝐙,ν)𝑛𝐗𝐙𝜈n(\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Z},\nu) of the whole population and 5) >=80absent80>=80 percentile of n​(𝐗,𝐙,ν)𝑛𝐗𝐙𝜈n(\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Z},\nu) of the whole population. Under each category, the consumers are assigned to have the same credit limit which equals to the median value of n​(𝐗,𝐙,ν)𝑛𝐗𝐙𝜈n(\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Z},\nu) of that category.

In this section, we provide detailed proofs of the Theorem stated in the main paper. Our goal is to construct score functions which satisfy the moment condition and the orthogonal condition concurrently such that we can recover the desired estimators. The two conditions are stated as follows:

The orthogonal score functions which can be used to recover the estimators (hence, the estimates) of θisuperscript𝜃𝑖\theta^{i}, θi∣jsuperscript𝜃conditional𝑖𝑗\theta^{i\mid j}, θi,ksuperscript𝜃𝑖𝑘\theta^{i,k} and θi,k∣jsuperscript𝜃𝑖conditional𝑘𝑗\theta^{i,k\mid j} are summarized in Theorem 3.2 in the main paper. Theorem 3.2 is restated here as the Theorem 7.3.

The proof of Theorem 7.3 makes use of the following result from (Chernozhukov, Newey, and Robins 2018):

Let X=(D,𝐔,𝐗,𝐙)𝑋𝐷𝐔𝐗𝐙X=(D,\mathbf{U},\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Z}) such that W=(Y,X)=(Y,D,𝐔,𝐗,𝐙)𝑊𝑌𝑋𝑌𝐷𝐔𝐗𝐙W=(Y,X)=(Y,D,\mathbf{U},\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Z}).We only need to prove (a) and (b) in the main paper. The proof is divided into two parts.

Proof of (a):

From the (2a) in the main paper, let X=(D,𝐔,𝐗,𝐙)𝑋𝐷𝐔𝐗𝐙X=(D,\mathbf{U},\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Z}). We also assume that m​(X,ℊ):=ℊ​(di,𝐔,𝐙)assign𝑚𝑋ℊℊsuperscript𝑑𝑖𝐔𝐙m(X,\mathcal{g}):=\mathcal{g}(d^{i},\mathbf{U},\mathbf{Z}). Therefore, we know thatΘisubscriptΘ𝑖\Theta_{i} is a convex set such that it equals {ϑi=𝔼​[ℊ​(di,𝐔,𝐙)]∣ℊ​i​s​ℙ​-​i​n​t​e​g​r​a​b​l​e}conditional-setsuperscriptitalic-ϑ𝑖𝔼delimited-[]ℊsuperscript𝑑𝑖𝐔𝐙ℊ𝑖𝑠ℙ-𝑖𝑛𝑡𝑒𝑔𝑟𝑎𝑏𝑙𝑒\{\vartheta^{i}=\mathbb{E}\left[\mathcal{g}(d^{i},\mathbf{U},\mathbf{Z})\right]\mid\mathcal{g}\;is\;\mathbb{P}\text{-}integrable\}Using Result 1, we show that ℊ↦𝔼​[m​(X,ℊ)]:=𝔼​[ℊ​(di,𝐔,𝐙)]maps-toℊ𝔼delimited-[]𝑚𝑋ℊassign𝔼delimited-[]ℊsuperscript𝑑𝑖𝐔𝐙\mathcal{g}\mapsto\mathbb{E}\left[m(X,\mathcal{g})\right]:=\mathbb{E}\left[\mathcal{g}(d^{i},\mathbf{U},\mathbf{Z})\right] is linear. ∎

In the section “Proofs of Theorem”, we present the score functions which can recover the IwC estimators of θisuperscript𝜃𝑖\theta^{i} and θi∣jsuperscript𝜃conditional𝑖𝑗\theta^{i\mid j}. In this section, we give out the score functions which are used to construct the IoC estimators and the doubly robust estimators (DREs) of θisuperscript𝜃𝑖\theta^{i} and θi∣jsuperscript𝜃conditional𝑖𝑗\theta^{i\mid j}, and check whether the score functions violate the orthogonal condition. As a result, we explain the differences between IoC estimators, DREs and IwC estimators from the theoretical standpoint. The results are summarized in the Proposition 7.4 and the Proposition 7.5.

To start with, we state the score functions that can recover the IoC estimators of θisuperscript𝜃𝑖\theta^{i} and θi∣jsuperscript𝜃conditional𝑖𝑗\theta^{i\mid j}. We can obtain the IoC estimators (the corresponding IoC estimations are computed using (3)) in the main paper. Clearly, the locally biasedness comes from the estimation of g​(di,⋅,⋅)𝑔superscript𝑑𝑖⋅⋅g(d^{i},\cdot,\cdot) and we need to have a good estimation of g​(di,⋅,⋅)𝑔superscript𝑑𝑖⋅⋅g(d^{i},\cdot,\cdot). Otherwise, the IoC estimators can be biased and lead to wrong conclusion in the causal inference analysis.

Next, we consider the score functions of θisuperscript𝜃𝑖\theta^{i} and θi∣jsuperscript𝜃conditional𝑖𝑗\theta^{i\mid j} that we can use to recover the corresponding DREs respectively.According to (Farrell 2015), the score function which can be used to recover the DRE of θisuperscript𝜃𝑖\theta^{i} isϑ−ℊ​(di,𝐔,𝐙)−𝟏{D=di}ai​(𝐗,𝐙)​(Y−ℊ​(di,𝐔,𝐙))italic-ϑℊsuperscript𝑑𝑖𝐔𝐙subscript1𝐷superscript𝑑𝑖subscript𝑎𝑖𝐗𝐙𝑌ℊsuperscript𝑑𝑖𝐔𝐙\displaystyle\vartheta-\mathcal{g}(d^{i},\mathbf{U},\mathbf{Z})-\frac{\mathbf{1}_{\{D=d^{i}\}}}{a_{i}(\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Z})}(Y-\mathcal{g}(d^{i},\mathbf{U},\mathbf{Z}))(27)while the score function which can be used to recover the DRE of θi∣jsuperscript𝜃conditional𝑖𝑗\theta^{i\mid j} isϑ−𝟏{D=dj}​ℊ​(di,𝐔,𝐙)mj−𝟏{D=di}​aj​(𝐗,𝐙)mj​ai​(𝐗,𝐙)​(Y−ℊ​(di,𝐔,𝐙)).italic-ϑsubscript1𝐷superscript𝑑𝑗ℊsuperscript𝑑𝑖𝐔𝐙subscript𝑚𝑗subscript1𝐷superscript𝑑𝑖subscript𝑎𝑗𝐗𝐙subscript𝑚𝑗subscript𝑎𝑖𝐗𝐙𝑌ℊsuperscript𝑑𝑖𝐔𝐙\displaystyle\vartheta-\frac{\mathbf{1}_{\{D=d^{j}\}}\mathcal{g}(d^{i},\mathbf{U},\mathbf{Z})}{m_{j}}-\frac{\mathbf{1}_{\{D=d^{i}\}}a_{j}(\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Z})}{m_{j}a_{i}(\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Z})}(Y-\mathcal{g}(d^{i},\mathbf{U},\mathbf{Z})).(28)In the Proposition 7.5, we check that if the score functions satisfy both the moment condition and the orthogonal condition. The results are summarized in the Proposition 7.5.

From (27), we notice that the score function which is used to recover the DRE of θisuperscript𝜃𝑖\theta^{i} is the same as the score function which is used to recover the IwC estimator of θisuperscript𝜃𝑖\theta^{i}. The moment condition and the orthogonal condition have been checked (see the proofs of (a) of the Theorem 3.2).

It remains to check the moment condition and the orthogonal condition of (28). Letψi∣j:=ϑ−𝟏{D=dj}​ℊ​(di,𝐔,𝐙)mj−𝟏{D=di}​aj​(𝐗,𝐙)mj​ai​(𝐗,𝐙)​(Y−ℊ​(di,𝐔,𝐙)).assignsuperscript𝜓conditional𝑖𝑗italic-ϑsubscript1𝐷superscript𝑑𝑗ℊsuperscript𝑑𝑖𝐔𝐙subscript𝑚𝑗subscript1𝐷superscript𝑑𝑖subscript𝑎𝑗𝐗𝐙subscript𝑚𝑗subscript𝑎𝑖𝐗𝐙𝑌ℊsuperscript𝑑𝑖𝐔𝐙\displaystyle\psi^{i\mid j}:=\vartheta-\frac{\mathbf{1}_{\{D=d^{j}\}}\mathcal{g}(d^{i},\mathbf{U},\mathbf{Z})}{m_{j}}-\frac{\mathbf{1}_{\{D=d^{i}\}}a_{j}(\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Z})}{m_{j}a_{i}(\mathbf{X},\mathbf{Z})}(Y-\mathcal{g}(d^{i},\mathbf{U},\mathbf{Z})).Again, we divide into two parts.

When using the DRE of θi∣jsuperscript𝜃conditional𝑖𝑗\theta^{i\mid j}, it requires the estimation of the quantity 𝔼​[𝟏{D=dj}]𝔼delimited-[]subscript1𝐷superscript𝑑𝑗\mathbb{E}\left[\mathbf{1}_{\{D=d^{j}\}}\right], which cause the locally biasedness of the DRE of θi∣jsuperscript𝜃conditional𝑖𝑗\theta^{i\mid j}. This is different from our IwC estimator of θi∣jsuperscript𝜃conditional𝑖𝑗\theta^{i\mid j}, which do not require the estimation of 𝔼​[𝟏{D=dj}]𝔼delimited-[]subscript1𝐷superscript𝑑𝑗\mathbb{E}\left[\mathbf{1}_{\{D=d^{j}\}}\right].

In this section we study the consistency of our IwC estimators of θisuperscript𝜃𝑖\theta^{i} and θi∣jsuperscript𝜃conditional𝑖𝑗\theta^{i\mid j} by decomposing the error in estimating the ‘true’ quantity using the IwC estimators. There are several advantages of using the error decomposition: 1. We can understand which terms contribute the most in estimating the true quantity 𝔼​[g​(di,𝐔,𝐙)]𝔼delimited-[]𝑔superscript𝑑𝑖𝐔𝐙\mathbb{E}\left[g(d^{i},\mathbf{U},\mathbf{Z})\right] or 𝔼​[g​(di,𝐔,𝐙)∣D=dj]𝔼delimited-[]conditional𝑔superscript𝑑𝑖𝐔𝐙𝐷superscript𝑑𝑗\mathbb{E}\left[g(d^{i},\mathbf{U},\mathbf{Z})\mid D=d^{j}\right]; 2. We can understand if our estimators are consistent estimators.Lemma 7.6 would help us determine if an estimator is consistent.

The proof of the Lemma 7.6 is omitted. Besides, there are several Lemmas that are useful when we want to prove the consistency of an estimator.
